            interstitial: googletag.sizeMapping()
                .addSize([0, 0], [640, 480])
                .build()
        };

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.201601474')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="e330f196b84d40d2804c98429eac1ed9"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e330f196b84d40d2804c98429eac1ed9">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'e150ae17-30f1-4d8b-ae9f-7ecda27abb92'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = 'E54BBD16D71612867290F90B0592B388';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')

                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);

                     <p>Collaborative Innovation Center of Chemical Science and Engineering (Tianjin), Tianjin, 300071 China</p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:jiaolf@nankai.edu.cn"><span>jiaolf@nankai.edu.cn</span></a><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Jiao/Lifang">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>
   </div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d87405523" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>The design of sodium ion batteries is proposed based on the use of flexible membrane composed of ultrasmall transition metal oxides. In this paper, the preparation of CuO quantum dots (≈2 nm) delicately embedded in carbon nanofibers (denoted as 2-CuO@C) with a thin film via a feasible electrospinning method is reported. The CuO content can be controlled by altering the synthetic conditions and is optimized to 54 wt%. As binder-free anode for sodium ion batteries, 2-CuO@C delivers an initial reversible capacity of 528 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at the current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, an exceptional rate capability of 250 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 5000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and an ultra-stable capacity of 401 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles at 500 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The enhancement of electrochemical performance is attributed to the unique structure of 2-CuO@C, which offers a variety of advantages: highly conductive carbon matrix suppressing agglomeration of CuO grains, interconnected nanofibers ensuring short transport length for electrons, well-dispersed CuO quantum dots leading to highly utilization rate, and good mechanical properties resulting in strong electrode integrity.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201601474-sec-0010">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201601474-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) as a complementary alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have received a great deal of attention due to the natural abundance of Na sources.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0001_R_d87405506e322" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Nanotechnol. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">1</a></span> Although the intercalation mechanism of sodium in electrochemical process is similar to that of lithium, the larger size of Na<sup>+</sup> ion (0.106 nm in radius) as compared to Li<sup>+</sup> ion (0.076 nm in radius) makes it difficult to explore suitable and stable electrode materials.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0002_R_d87405506e328" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Rev. Small J. Electrochem. Soc. Nano Lett.">2</a></span> Particularly, appropriate anode materials with high capacity and excellent rate capability are still limited.</p>
         
         <p>As an effective strategy, transition metal oxides (TMO) with conversion reactions (M<span class="icomoon"></span>O, M = Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Cu) have been developed owing to their high gravimetric specific capacity.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0003_R_d87405506e333" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nano Lett. Chem. Commun. Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys. Chem. Mater. Chem. Commun. Adv. Mater. Interfaces">3</a></span> Among them, cupric oxide (CuO) is an attractive candidate by the virtue of its huge availability, low cost, environmental friendliness, and high theoretical capacity (674 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0004_R_d87405506e337" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nanoscale Adv. Mater.">4</a></span> Unfortunately, electrochemical conversion reactions are usually accompanied with large volume variation and structure collapse, leading to a rapid deterioration of the cyclability.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0005_R_d87405506e339" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></span> To solve this problem, great efforts have been made to fabricate nanoscale CuO to mitigate the absolute strain generated upon sodiation/desodiation process.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0006_R_d87405506e341" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Small">6</a></span> It is worth mentioning that the effect of particle size is very important for both fundamental research and industry production. For example, Wang et al. synthesized porous CuO nanowires through decomposition of Cu(OH)<sub>2</sub> precursor and obtained a capacity of 303 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> for 50 cycles at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0007_R_d87405506e350" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Res.">7</a></span> Although nanomaterials can certainly improve the Na-storage ability, the cycling performance is still far away from meeting large-scale applications because of the aggregation of nano-CuO with high surface energy and the rate performance is also unsatisfactory due to the poor conductivity of CuO. Correspondingly, dispersing nanoparticles in carbon matrix could significantly suppress the particle agglomeration, accommodate the volume expansion, and enhance the electric conductivity.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0008_R_d87405506e352" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Nano Res.">8</a></span> Specifically, Chen's group successfully fabricated CuO/C composites (CuO size ≈10 nm, content 44 wt%) using aerosol spray pyrolysis, which displayed a discharge capacity of 426 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0009_R_d87405506e359" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nanoscale">9</a></span> However, the CuO/C composite which simultaneously controls the CuO particle size to less than 5 nm and ensures the CuO content to more than 50 wt% has never been reported, yet. Considerable improvements in the design and optimization of CuO/C nanostructure are still required.</p>
         
         <p>1D nanostructure can effectively decrease the ionic diffusion length and enhance electronic transport along their geometry, leading to fast diffusion kinetics.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0010_R_d87405506e364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">10</a></span> Electrospinning is a feasible technique to prepare various 1D carbon-containing composites.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0011_R_d87405506e366" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater. ACS Nano">11</a></span> This method can also produce flexible membrane, which can be directly used as working electrodes without adding polymer binders and conductive agent.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0012_R_d87405506e368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></span> As we all know, organic polymeric binder would result in poor electronic conductivity and irreversible capacity fading.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0013_R_d87405506e370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Small Nanoscale">13</a></span> Synthesis of binder-free anode materials for rechargeable SIBs can not only benefit the electrochemical performance, but also decrease the costs of batteries. For example, binder-free CuO arrays were fabricated by in situ engraving the copper foil, and a capacity of 290.6 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 450 cycles at 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0014_R_d87405506e377" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Herein, aiming at inexpensive production cost and excellent cycling stability, we report on the preparation of CuO quantum dots (≈2 nm) uniformly encapsulated in carbon nanofibers (denoted as 2-CuO@C) via a facile electrospinning method. We also realize controllable preparation for the size and content of CuO and is optimized to 54 wt%. This tailored 2-CuO@C is directly used as binder-free anode for SIBs, exhibiting high reversible capacity (528 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at the current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), high rate capability (250 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> even at 5000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), and high cycle stability (a capacity of 401 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 500 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, Coulombic efficiency (CE) is close to 100% after 500 cycles), showing great potential for practical application. The enhancement of electrochemical performance is due to the following merits: the ultrasmall size of CuO quantum dots can significantly improve the utilization rate of electrode materials, the 1D carbon matrix can prevent aggregation of CuO grains during the prolonged sodiation/desodiation process, the binder-free electrodes can enhance the electronic conductivity and reduce the irreversible capacity losses.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201601474-sec-0020">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201601474-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll201601474-sec-0030">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll201601474-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structure Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The preparation details are shown in <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0006">1</a>. In this process, the cupric ions facilitate the formation of carbon and carbon frame confines the growth of CuO grains, thus CuO quantum dots uniformly embedded in carbon matrix was successfully synthesized. Correspondingly, the morphology of composites and the size of CuO particles can be controlled by changing the precursor solution composition and reaction conditions.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201601474-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0192fd89-0cd9-47ad-8272-3e0aeeee63f2/smll201601474-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0192fd89-0cd9-47ad-8272-3e0aeeee63f2/smll201601474-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/03aa91b4-b415-4238-ba1f-e9459a036a30/smll201601474-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0192fd89-0cd9-47ad-8272-3e0aeeee63f2/smll201601474-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201601474-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201601474" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the formation process for CuO@C nanofibers.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0001">1</a>a shows the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of the as-prepared 2-CuO@C, 20-CuO@C, and bulk-CuO. All the diffraction peaks of these samples correspond to a C2/c space group of monoclinic crystal system CuO (JCPDS File No. 45-0937). The broad diffraction peak positioned at 24° can be assigned to the carbon phase.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0015_R_d87405506e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Lett. ACS Nano">15</a></span> No peaks of impurities were observed, demonstrating that CuO@C with high purity has been successfully synthesized by the facile electrospinning method. The Raman spectra were applied to further certify the existence of carbon. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0001">1</a>b, two characteristic peaks, which are located at 1340 and 1590 cm<sup>−1</sup>, can be attributed to the typical disorder-induced feature (D bands) and the E<sub>2g</sub> patterns of graphite (G bands) of carbon, respectively.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0015_R_d87405506e454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nano Lett. ACS Nano">15</a></span> Moreover, the peaks around 287, 332, and 627 cm<sup>−1</sup> are CuO signals.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0009_R_d87405506e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nanoscale">9</a></span> Significantly, XRD patterns and Raman spectra of the pure carbon nanofibers (CNFs) are displayed in Figure S1 (Supporting Information). Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) in Figure S2 (Supporting Information) reveals that the mass ratio of CuO in 2-CuO@C and 20-CuO@C composites is ≈54% and 83%, respectively.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201601474-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fe4e61be-7df7-4a33-b598-731393014412/smll201601474-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fe4e61be-7df7-4a33-b598-731393014412/smll201601474-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d3456378-0e2e-4a9c-a4a0-b9a65bda35d8/smll201601474-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fe4e61be-7df7-4a33-b598-731393014412/smll201601474-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201601474-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201601474" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of the as-prepared 2-CuO@C, 20-CuO@C, bulk-CuO and the standard card of CuO (JCPDS No. 45-937). b) Raman spectra of the 2-CuO@C, 20-CuO@C, and bulk-CuO.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0002">2</a>a,b present the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of 2-CuO@C and 20-CuO@C. The fibers of two samples are both smooth and continuous with the length up to a hundreds of micrometers, and the average diameter of 2-CuO@C and 20-CuO@C is ≈80 and 280 nm, respectively, which well interlink into a conductive 3D network. This structure would facilitate the contact between active materials and electrolyte, leading to rapid charge and discharge.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0016_R_d87405506e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></span> Indeed, different reaction conditions were attempted to optimize the morphology. Figure S3 (Supporting Information) presents the SEM images of CuCl<sub>2</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O@PAN nanofibers with different CuCl<sub>2</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O contents. We can see that a lower concentration of CuCl<sub>2</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O leads to a fine nanofiber, while a higher concentration of CuCl<sub>2</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O results in uneven morphology. However, the rare distribution of CuO will not benefit for the electrochemical performance.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0017_R_d87405506e502" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></span> The SEM images of composites in different carbonization temperature are shown in Figure S4 (Supporting Information). Although a lower temperature (600 °C) leads to an uniform 1D morphology, it will result in poor electrical conductivity due to the incomplete carbonization, just as illustrated in Figure S10 (Supporting Information).<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0018_R_d87405506e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem. A Energy Environ. Sci.">18</a></span> Obviously, the higher temperatures (700, 750 °C) induce some agglomeration of the composite nanofibers. Considering the uniform morphology of nanofibers, 650 °C should be the most appropriate carbonization temperature.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201601474-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bb7ae1a1-7a66-4fbf-a6d8-59eca505e93d/smll201601474-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/bb7ae1a1-7a66-4fbf-a6d8-59eca505e93d/smll201601474-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8659135b-2a07-429c-a101-547c4d712bd2/smll201601474-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bb7ae1a1-7a66-4fbf-a6d8-59eca505e93d/smll201601474-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201601474-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201601474" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of a) 2-CuO@C and b) 20-CuO@C; TEM images of c,e) 2-CuO@C and d,f) 20-CuO@C (inset: corresponding HRTEM images of 2-CuO@C). g) TEM (HAADF Cu O C N) elemental mapping images of 2-CuO@C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0002">2</a>c–f shows different resolution transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images of the two samples, CuO quantum dots (black dots) are delicately embedded in carbon nanofibers (gray color). The diagrams of particle size distribution in Figure S5 (Supporting Information) (by surveying 100 CuO nanoparticles using Nano-Measure software) manifest that CuO grains in 2-CuO@C are around 2 nm, while the CuO particles in 20-CuO@C are 20 nm with some aggregation. As far as we know, the composite which simultaneously controls the smallest CuO quantum dots and ensures the sufficient CuO content has never been reported. The HRTEM image (inset of Figure <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0002">2</a>e) shows a group of parallel fringes with a <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.25 nm clearly, corresponding to the crystal (002) planes of monoclinic CuO.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0007_R_d87405506e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Res.">7</a>, <a href="#smll201601474-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0009_R_d87405506e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nanoscale">9</a>, <a href="#smll201601474-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0014_R_d87405506e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></span> This result is consistent with the XRD patterns. Figure <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0002">2</a>g shows the TEM elemental mapping images of 2-CuO@C, revealing that CuO nanoparticles are homogeneously located in the carbon matrix, where the content of CuO is ≈54 wt% as shown in Figure S2 (Supporting Information). The stable carbon frame and the uniform distribution of CuO quantum dots can effectively prevent CuO nanoparticles from pulverization and aggregation during cycling, thereby benefiting the electrochemical performance.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0019_R_d87405506e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">19</a></span> Moreover, TEM images of 2-CuO@C composites carbonized at 650 °C for different time are also given in Figure S6 (Supporting Information). In combination with these samples XRD patterns (Figure S7, Supporting Information), it is found that a short duration time (0.5 h) cannot effectively drive the decomposition of CuCl<sub>2</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O, while longer duration time will result in aggregation of CuO particles. Bulk-CuO was prepared for comparison, just as shown in Figure S8 (Supporting Information). Additionally, Figure S9 (Supporting Information) presents SEM and TEM images of pure CNFs.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll201601474-sec-0040">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll201601474-sec-0040-title">2.2 Na Storage Mechanism and Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>Na half cells were assembled to elucidate the electrochemical reactive processes of CuO@C nanofibers. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0003">3</a>a displays typical cyclic voltammograms (CV) curves of 2-CuO@C composite at a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> between 0.01 and 3.0 V. It is found that the first cycle is extremely different from other two cycles, specifically in the sodiation process. During the first sodiation process, a wide current peak at 0.87 V is ascribed to the formation of solid-electrolyte interface (SEI) layer and the decomposition of organic electrolyte. The peaks located at 1.76, 0.49, and 0.12 V in subsequent sodiation process represent three reversible electrochemical reactions: Na<sup>+</sup> insert into CuO interlayer to form Cu<sup>II</sup><sub>1−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Cu<sup>I</sup><i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>1−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i><sub>/2</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>O, Cu<sup>II</sup><sub>1−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> Cu<sup>I</sup><i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>1−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i><sub>/2</sub> reacts with Na<sup>+</sup> to generate Cu<sub>2</sub>O phase and finally Cu<sub>2</sub>O decomposed into Na<sub>2</sub>O and Cu. During the desodiation process, two well-defined peaks at 1.29 and 2.27 V are attributed to the formation of Cu<sub>2</sub>O and CuO, respectively.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0007_R_d87405506e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nano Res.">7</a>, <a href="#smll201601474-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0009_R_d87405506e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nanoscale">9</a>, <a href="#smll201601474-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0014_R_d87405506e606" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></span></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201601474-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/87787092-1d08-4dda-9505-9e6802fb5c20/smll201601474-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/87787092-1d08-4dda-9505-9e6802fb5c20/smll201601474-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/11779eae-3b5a-4aa3-9c79-55593bbaef79/smll201601474-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/87787092-1d08-4dda-9505-9e6802fb5c20/smll201601474-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201601474-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201601474" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cyclic voltammograms of 2-CuO@C between 0.01 and 3.0 V at a rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) XPS spectra of reaction products at different charge and discharge states. c) Charge/discharge curves of 2-CuO@C at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. HRTEM images of the electrode after first fully d) discharged and f) charged process. e) Schematic illustration for the mechanism of 2-CuO@C nanofibers during repeated sodiation/desodiation.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>In order to further confirm the phase evolution of CuO during sodiation/desodiation process, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was performed at selected charged/discharged states between 0.01 and 3.0 V (current density = 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). Figure <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0003">3</a>b shows XPS patterns of 2-CuO@C electrode at the designated potentials. The XPS peaks of fresh electrode are well indexed to Cu<sup>2+</sup> (934.9, 934.1, 933.5 eV), while no peaks of Cu<sup>+</sup> (932.9 eV) can be found, indicating the high purity of the products. In the discharge process, the peak intensity of Cu<sup>2+</sup> is decreased and new peak of Cu<sup>+</sup> is observed. After discharge to 0.01 V, clear Cu<sup>0</sup> (932.6 eV) peak can be found, verifying the reduction reaction from CuO to Cu<sup>II</sup><sub>1−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>Cu<sup>I</sup><i><sub>x</sub></i>O<sub>1−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i><sub>/2</sub>, Cu<sub>2</sub>O, and further formation of Cu.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0014_R_d87405506e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#smll201601474-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0020_R_d87405506e664" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Acta Mater.">20</a></span> Obviously, the almost vanishing of the peak of Cu<sup>2+</sup> implies that CuO quantum dots can be fully utilized to store sodium. During the process of charge, the typical peak of Cu<sup>0</sup> gradually disappears and Cu<sup>+</sup> emerges. With a fully charge, the characteristic peaks of Cu<sup>2+</sup> recovers, corresponding to the oxidation reaction from Cu to Cu<sub>2</sub>O and CuO.</p>
            
            <p>Additionally, the galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of 2-CuO@C are investigated at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The obtained peaks in charge/discharge curves are greatly consistent with CV results, just as shown in Figure <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0003">3</a>c. In the first cycle, 2-CuO@C delivers the discharge and charge capacity of 828 and 555 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to a CE of 67%. The considerable capacity loss during the first cycle is mainly resulted from the formation of SEI layer and the side reactions of organic electrolyte. Obviously, in the following cycles, either the charge or discharge curves of 2-CuO@C are well overlapped, which manifests that the 2-CuO@C electrode possesses high capacity retention. Figure <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0003">3</a>d,f presents the HRTEM images of the 2-CuO@C electrode reaction products after the first charge/discharge (discharged to 0.01 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na and charged to 3.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) curves. Significantly, the discharge product is well assigned to metallic copper with the (111) interplanar spacing of 0.20 nm. The particle size of Cu has not changed much, confirming that CuO quantum dots encapsulated in stable carbon framework can effectively relieve volume change and keep CuO from growing bigger during sodiation. When charged to 3.0 V, the Cu transforms into CuO phase. Displaying a clear lattice fringes with a <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.25 nm, which is well in agreement with (002) plane of crystalline CuO. These results are consistent with the XPS. The electrochemical conversion reactions mechanism is provided in Figure <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0003">3</a>e and details are as follows (Equations <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll201601474-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>–<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll201601474-disp-0005" title="Link to equation">5</a>).</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">During the discharge process
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201601474-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/d0af2f7d-0772-46f2-b902-0c1f6c32df7e/smll201601474-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201601474:smll201601474-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201601474:smll201601474-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201601474-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/b11e1126-d2dc-46a3-b348-50119f5a5a9b/smll201601474-math-0002.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201601474:smll201601474-math-0002" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201601474:smll201601474-math-0002" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201601474-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/3c2ae09e-a696-40c9-8f31-8e7030ae5791/smll201601474-math-0003.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201601474:smll201601474-math-0003" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201601474:smll201601474-math-0003" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">During the charge process
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201601474-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/244171d8-3451-41b7-b31e-9ac0ade2252b/smll201601474-math-0004.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201601474:smll201601474-math-0004" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201601474:smll201601474-math-0004" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201601474-disp-0005"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/f1cf07fc-4a74-478a-80bb-2705507ed95e/smll201601474-math-0005.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201601474:smll201601474-math-0005" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201601474:smll201601474-math-0005" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(5)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>For the evaluation of the cycling stability of 2-CuO@C, 20-CuO@C, and bulk-CuO, the electrode was galvanostatic charged and discharged at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> for 100 cycles (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0004">4</a>a). The reversible capacity of 2-CuO@C composite is perfectly maintained compared with other two samples. After a few cycles stabilization, the reversible capacity can reach 528 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, a high capacity retention of 83% can still be received over 100 cycles. In comparison, 20-CuO@C exhibits the reversible capacity of 465 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after few cycles and remains 242 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles, while bulk-CuO displays a fast capacity loss. The higher capacity retention of 2-CuO@C indicates the uniform dispersion of quantum dots in carbon matrix could generate a balanced stress upon prolonged cycling and maintain the integrity of electrode. Furthermore, the electrochemical performance of CNFs is shown in Figure S10 (Supporting Information). It exhibits excellent rate capability (107, 101, 96, 85, 77, and 68 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 100, 300, 500, 1000, 3000, and 5000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). Such remarkable electrochemical behavior and firm structure are beneficial to the CuO@C composites.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201601474-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ed6ee5ba-4736-4999-9ebc-b5aed3c0610e/smll201601474-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ed6ee5ba-4736-4999-9ebc-b5aed3c0610e/smll201601474-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2579a104-a85e-4dfb-8637-366d84d5c74a/smll201601474-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ed6ee5ba-4736-4999-9ebc-b5aed3c0610e/smll201601474-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201601474-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201601474" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cycling performances of the three samples at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. b) Rate capability of 2-CuO@C and 20-CuO@C at different current densities. c) Cycling performance of 2-CuO@C at a current density of 500 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0004">4</a>b displays rate capability of as-prepared CuO@C samples at various current densities from 100 to 5000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. In the initial ten cycles, the 2-CuO@C electrode exhibits a reversible capacity as high as 530 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. Then, the current density is gradually increased to 300, 500, 1000, and 3000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the corresponding capacities can reach 495, 450, 384, and 315 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Even at a high current rate of 5000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, it also delivers nearly 250 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. More importantly, when the current density is switched from 5000 to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the desodiation/sodiation capacity swiftly recovers to 477 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> within few cycles. The exceptional rate performance is owing to the 1D carbon nanofibers connected together to form 3D network, which could effectively facilitate the ionic/electron diffusion kinetics.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0021_R_d87405506e781" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small">21</a></span> Furthermore, the CuO quantum dots are readily accessible to the electrolyte and facile to be fully utilized for sodium-storage and the binder-free electrodes could improve the electronic conductivity, rendering the high rate capability.<span><a href="#smll201601474-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201601474-bib-0022_R_d87405506e783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Small Adv. Funct. Mater.">22</a></span> By contrast, the 20-CuO@C displays an inferior rate performance. The maximum reversible capacities obtained at 100, 300, 500, 1000, 3000, and 5000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> are 460, 330, 256, 189, 158, and 121 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. After 60 cycles, when the current density is back to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, only a capacity of 308 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was retained. The large CuO particles of 20-CuO@C could not endure the stress derived from rapid Na ion insertion and extraction. They would pulverize to some extent, resulting in a deterioration of the cyclability. In addition, the effects of particle size on electric conductivity are specifically elucidated by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) (Figure S11, Supporting Information). As can be concluded, the smallest particle size can significantly facilitate the electron transfer and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.</p>
            
            <p>Another perfect feature of 2-CuO@C electrode is the long-term cycle stability at high current density. Figure <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0004">4</a>c shows the cycling performance and the CE of 2-CuO@C at 500 mA g<sup>−1</sup> for 500 cycles. The specific capacities at the end of 50, 100, 200, 300, 400, and 500 cycles still retain 421, 416, 409, 405, 403, and 401 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to a negligible capacity loss for each cycle. The exceptional rate capability and cycling stability of 2-CuO@C are summarized in <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll201601474-tbl-0001">1</a>, which is superior to the previous reports about CuO anodes for SIBs. The enhancement of electrochemical performance is ascribed to the following merits: first, the ultrasmall CuO quantum dots can significantly improve the utilization rate of active materials and tolerate the absolute strain during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction process. Second, the uniform dispersion of CuO grains in carbon nanofibers can accommodate the large volume variation and prevent the particle aggregation over repeated sodiation/desodiation processes. Finally, the 1D carbon frame can interlink into a 3D conductive network, no more conductive carbon and binder are needed, which would facilitate the electrons transport and Na ions diffusion kinetics.</p>

                    </span>Comparison of the results in this study with previously reported performance of CuO anode for SIBs</header>

                           <th class="bottom-bordered-cell center-aligned">Cycle stability</th>

                           </tr>
                        </thead>
                     
                     <tbody>

                           </tr>

                           </tr>
                        </tbody>
                     </table>
               </div>
               
               
               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>To further understand the difference in the electrochemical performance between 2-CuO@C and 20-CuO@C, SEM and TEM analyses have been used to observe the morphological and structural changes after long-term cycling (over 100 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). As revealed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201601474-fig-0005">5</a>, the nanofibers of 2-CuO@C still maintained its original appearance after intensive cycling. The results demonstrate that the hybrid material with unique structure of ultrafine CuO finely confined in the carbon matrix can offer a suitable elastic buffer space to accommodate the expansion of CuO particles, thus greatly enhancing rate performance and ensuring long-cycle life. In contrast, 20-CuO@C nanocomposite was collapsed in some degree and most CuO nanoparticles are pulverized as well as aggregated, which should explain the rapid capacity losses of 20-CuO@C.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201601474-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a9c5010b-4e7b-420b-bb55-e06b260faf93/smll201601474-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a9c5010b-4e7b-420b-bb55-e06b260faf93/smll201601474-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/22060c27-5d5d-41ba-9790-b7cc79b1237a/smll201601474-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a9c5010b-4e7b-420b-bb55-e06b260faf93/smll201601474-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201601474-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201601474" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM and TEM images of a,c) 2-CuO@C and b,d) 20-CuO@C after 100 charge/discharge cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201601474-sec-0050">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201601474-sec-0050-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, CuO quantum dots (≈2 nm) uniformly embedded in carbon nanofibers were prepared by a simple and scalable electrospinning technique, and we also realize controllable preparation for the size and content of CuO, this not only can be applied to the metal oxides but also to all kinds of active materials. More importantly, this tailored 2-CuO@C can be directly used as binder- and current collector-free anode for SIBs, delivering a reversible capacity as high as 528 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at the current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Significantly, the capacity of 250 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> even at 5000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and 401 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> over 500 cycles at 500 mA g<sup>−1</sup> were achieved. To our knowledge, this is the best cycling stability and rate performance of cupric oxide for Na storage. The enhanced electrochemical performance further demonstrates nano-CuO has great potential for practical application, especially in portable energy storage devices. We believe that this simple synthesis method as well as the outstanding electrochemical performance would provide a certain reference to the relevant materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201601474-sec-0060">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201601474-sec-0060-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Materials Synthesis</i>: In the typical synthesis, polyacrylonitrile (PAN, average <i>M</i><sub>w</sub> = 150 000, Aldrich) was used as carbon source, <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide (DMF, anhydrous, 99.8%, Aldrich) as the solvent, and copper (II) chloride dihydrate (CuCl<sub>2</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O, 99%, Alfa Aesar) as the source of CuO. To prepare the precursor solution, 0.6 g PAN and 0.58 g CuCl<sub>2</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O were dissolved in 12 mL of DMF at 60 °C for 6 h under magnetic stirring. The mixture was transferred into 5 mL plastic syringe equipped with a 21-gauge blunt tip needle and electrospun on spinning system at 15 kV. The syringes were pushed at a speed of 0.6 mL h<sup>−1</sup>. A copper foil as the collector was located 15 cm away from the needle to collect a light-yellow film. Then, the as-collected film was first stabilized at 260 °C for 1 h and carbonized at 650 °C for 1 h in nitrogen atmosphere with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup>. Finally, the films were treated at 270 °C for 4 h in air to get 2-CuO@C with 2 nm CuO nanoparticles. For comparison, CuO@C composite with CuO nanoparticles of 20 nm (denoted as 20-CuO@C) was obtained through the same steps, only needing to change the precursor mass of CuCl<sub>2</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O to 0.91 g. Additionally, the bulk-CuO (denoted as bulk-CuO) was also synthesized by the same procedures except that the oxidation temperature was increased from 270 to 400 °C in order to eliminate the carbon.</p>
         
         <p><i>Materials Characterizations</i>: The crystal structure and purity of the materials were studied by powder X-ray diffraction recorded on a Rigaku D/Max-2500 using Cu Kα radiation (<i>λ</i> = 1.5408 Å). The morphology and microstructure were characterized by field-emission SEM (HITACHI S-4800) and TEM (Tecnai G2 F20). The carbon contents in the CuO@C composites were determined by using TG-DSC analyzer (NETZSCH, STA 449 F3) from 40 to 650 °C with a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>−1</sup> in air. Raman spectra were studied on a Renishaw inVia Raman spectrometer using laser excitation at 514.5 nm. XPS (Perkin Elmer PHI 1600 ESCA) was used to analyze the phase evolution of CuO.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Measurements</i>: The CuO@C films were cut into <i>d</i> = 12 mm circular samples to be directly used as working electrodes without adding any polymeric binder and conductive additives. The average weight of active material was about 1.5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> and the thickness of working electrodes was about 0.30 mm, which were mainly controlled by electrospinning time and flow rate of solution. The cells were assembled in an argon-filled glove box and the counter electrode was sodium metal. The electrolyte was 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC) and dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (EC:DMC = 1:1 by volume) and the separator was glass fiber. Galvanostatic charge–discharge tests at different current densities were examined in the voltage range of 0.01<b>–</b>3.0 V on a Land battery tester (CT2001A). Cyclic voltammetry at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> were performed on a CHI 660B electrochemical workstation (Chenhua, China) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy was measured at a frequency range from 100 kHz to 100 mHz. All the specific capacity in this paper was calculated based on the mass of CuO. In order to investigate the microstructure of these cycled materials, the cell was disassembled in an argon-filled glove box. Subsequently, the as-obtained electrode was rinsed with diethyl carbonate (DEC) several times and then sonicated in ethanol to prepare the TEM test samples.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
